<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Anthoceros genomes illuminate the origin of land plants and the unique biology of hornworts"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2020 6:3"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-03-13"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Hornworts comprise a bryophyte lineage that diverged from other extant land plants &gt;400&#8201;million years ago and bears unique biological features, including a distinct sporophyte architecture, cyanobacterial symbiosis and a pyrenoid-based carbon-concentrating mechanism (CCM). Here, we provide three high-quality genomes of Anthoceros hornworts. Phylogenomic analyses place hornworts as a sister clade to liverworts plus mosses with high support. The Anthoceros genomes lack repeat-dense centromeres as well as whole-genome duplication, and contain a limited transcription factor repertoire. Several genes involved in angiosperm meristem and stomatal function are conserved in Anthoceros and upregulated during sporophyte development, suggesting possible homologies at the genetic level. We identified candidate genes involved in cyanobacterial symbiosis and found that LCIB, a Chlamydomonas CCM gene, is present in hornworts but absent in other plant lineages, implying a possible conserved role in CCM function. We anticipate that these hornwort genomes will serve as essential references for future hornwort research and comparative studies across land plants. Analyses of three high-quality genomes of Anthoceros hornworts place hornworts as a sister clade to the lineage including liverworts and mosses, and provide insights into the unique biological features of hornworts."/>

Analyses of three high-quality genomes of Anthoceros hornworts place hornworts as a sister clade to the lineage including liverworts and mosses, and provide insights into the unique biological features of hornworts."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Hornworts comprise a bryophyte lineage that diverged from other extant land plants &gt;400 million years ago and bears unique biological features, including a distinct sporophyte architecture, cyanobacterial symbiosis and a pyrenoid-based carbon-concentrating mechanism (CCM). Here, we provide three high-quality genomes of <i>Anthoceros</i> hornworts. Phylogenomic analyses place hornworts as a sister clade to liverworts plus mosses with high support. The <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes lack repeat-dense centromeres as well as whole-genome duplication, and contain a limited transcription factor repertoire. Several genes involved in angiosperm meristem and stomatal function are conserved in <i>Anthoceros</i> and upregulated during sporophyte development, suggesting possible homologies at the genetic level. We identified candidate genes involved in cyanobacterial symbiosis and found that <i>LCIB</i>, a <i>Chlamydomonas</i> CCM gene, is present in hornworts but absent in other plant lineages, implying a possible conserved role in CCM function. We anticipate that these hornwort genomes will serve as essential references for future hornwort research and comparative studies across land plants.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Land plants evolved from a charophycean algal ancestor 470–515 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Morris, J. L. et al. The timescale of early land plant evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E2274–E2283 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1441">1</a></sup> and contributed to the greening of the terrestrial environment. The extant land plants consist of vascular plants and three bryophyte lineages—mosses, liverworts and hornworts. While the phylogeny of land plants has been debated, recent evidence indicates that bryophytes are monophyletic with hornworts a sister clade to Setaphyta (liverworts and mosses)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nishiyama, T. et al. Chloroplast phylogeny indicates that bryophytes are monophyletic. Mol. Biol. Evol. 21, 1813–1819 (2004)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1445">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wickett, N. J. et al. Phylotranscriptomic analysis of the origin and early diversification of land plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4859–E4868 (2014)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1445_1">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Puttick, M. N. et al. The interrelationships of land plants and the nature of the ancestral embryophyte. Curr. Biol. 28, 733–745 (2018)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1445_2">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="de Sousa, F., Foster, P. G., Donoghue, P. C. J., Schneider, H. &amp; Cox, C. J. Nuclear protein phylogenies support the monophyly of the three bryophyte groups (Bryophyta Schimp). New Phytol. 222, 565–575 (2019)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1445_3">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="One Thousand Plant Transcriptomes Initiative. One thousand plant transcriptomes and the phylogenomics of green plants. Nature 574, 679–685 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1448">6</a></sup>.</p><p>The evolution of land plants is underlined by the rise of morphological, molecular and physiological innovations. Tracing the evolutionary origins of these key innovations is prone to errors due to uncertainty in reconstructing the most recent common ancestor (MRCA) of land plants. More than 400 million years of independent evolution of the three bryophyte lineages have provided ample time for evolutionary changes to happen and the availability of model systems for only two bryophyte lineages—mosses (<i>Physcomitrella patens</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Rensing, S. A. et al. The Physcomitrella genome reveals evolutionary insights into the conquest of land by plants. Science 319, 64–69 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1458">7</a></sup> and liverworts (<i>Marchantia polymorpha</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287–299 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1465">8</a></sup>—makes inferences even more difficult. Hornworts, as the earliest diverging lineage in bryophytes, are crucial to infer character evolution and reveal the nature of the MRCA of bryophytes and that of land plants.</p><p>Hornworts uniquely possess a combination of traits that connect them with both green algae and other land plant lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Renzaglia, K. S. Comparative morphology and developmental anatomy of the Anthocerotophyta. J. Hattori Bot. Lab. 44, 31–90 (1978)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1472">9</a></sup>. For instance, most hornworts have a single chloroplast per cell with a pyrenoid capable of carrying out a carbon-concentrating mechanism (CCM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Smith, E. C. &amp; Griffiths, H. A pyrenoid-based carbon-concentrating mechanism is present in terrestrial bryophytes of the class Anthocerotae. Planta 200, 203–212 (1996)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1476">10</a></sup>. Such pyrenoid-based CCMs cannot be found in any other land plants but frequently occur in algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Li, F.-W., Villarreal Aguilar, J. C. &amp; Szövényi, P. Hornworts: an overlooked window into carbon-concentrating mechanisms. Trends Plant Sci. 22, 275–277 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1480">11</a></sup>. Conversely, hornwort sporophytes are long-lived and moderately independent of gametophytes, which have been assumed to be features linking them to vascular plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Qiu, Y.-L. et al. The deepest divergences in land plants inferred from phylogenomic evidence. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 15511–15516 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1484">12</a></sup>. Furthermore, hornwort sporophytes bear stomata that may be homologous with those of vascular plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Renzaglia, K. S., Villarreal Aguilar, J. C., Piatkowski, B. T., Lucas, J. R. &amp; Merced, A. Hornwort stomata: architecture and fate shared with 400-Million-year-old fossil plants without leaves. Plant Physiol. 174, 788–797 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1488">13</a></sup>.</p><p>In addition to having characteristics exclusively shared with algae or with other land plants, hornworts also have a wide range of distinctive biological features. For example, the presence of a basal sporophytic meristem and asynchronous meiosis are unique to hornworts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Renzaglia, K. S., Villarreal, J. C. &amp; Duff, R. J. in Bryophyte Biology Vol. 2 (eds Goffinet, B. &amp; Shaw, J.) 139–171 (Cambridge Univ. Press, 2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1495">14</a></sup>. Moreover, hornworts are among the very few plants that have a symbiotic relationship with nitrogen-fixing cyanobacteria<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Meeks, J. C. Symbiosis between nitrogen-fixing cyanobacteria and plants. Bioscience 48, 266–276 (1998)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1499">15</a></sup> and one particular hornwort species, <i>Anthoceros punctatus</i>, has been used as a model system to study plant–cyanobacteria interactions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Meeks, J. C. Physiological adaptations in nitrogen-fixing Nostoc–plant symbiotic associations. Microbiol. Monogr. 8, 181–205 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1506">16</a></sup>.</p><p>Detailed genomic information on hornworts is essential not only to understand the evolutionary assembly of land plant-specific traits, but also to substantiate the full potential of hornworts as a model for studying the genetic basis of cyanobacterial symbiosis and pyrenoid-based CCMs. Here, we provide three high-quality genome assemblies and their annotations for the genus <i>Anthoceros</i>. We use these data to refine our inferences on the nature of the land plant MRCA and to gain new insights into hornwort biology.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Genome assembly and annotation"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Genome assembly and annotation</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>We assembled three hornwort genomes from <i>Anthoceros agrestis</i> (Bonn and Oxford strains) and <i>A. punctatus</i>. For <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn, a combination of short- and long-read data with Chicago and Hi-C libraries resulted in a chromosomal-scale assembly with the six largest scaffolds containing 95% of the assembled genome (<i>A. agrestis</i> has six chromosome pairs; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">1</a>). For <i>A. agrestis</i> Oxford strain and <i>A. punctatus</i>, we used Oxford Nanopore sequencing to obtain high-quality assemblies composed of roughly 200 contigs with N50 over 1.7 megabase pairs (Mb) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Tab1">1</a>). The three genomes are highly collinear with a greater collinearity found between the two <i>A. agrestis</i> strains (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">2</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">1</a>). The collinearity, BUSCO (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">3</a>) and read mapping statistics (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">3</a>), show that the three genomes are of high quality and accuracy.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Genome organizations in the Anthoceros genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Genome organizations in the <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0618-2/MediaObjects/41477_2020_618_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0618-2/MediaObjects/41477_2020_618_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="794" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Circos plot of <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn showing the densities of repeats, genes and single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) with the <i>A. agrestis</i> Oxford genomes. <b>b</b>–<b>e</b>, <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes lack whole genome duplication. No self-synteny can be found in the three <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes (<i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn (<b>b</b>), <i>A. agrestis</i> Oxford (<b>c</b>) and <i>A. punctatus</i> (<b>d</b>)) nor in <i>M. polymorpha</i> (<b>e</b>). <b>f</b>, <i>P. patens</i>, on the other hand, shows a clear 1:1 and some 1:2 syntenic relationship, suggesting paleopolyploidy. In <b>b</b>–<b>f</b>, the bar graphs show the proportion of the genome at different self syntenic levels, with the dot-plots on the right.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly statistics of the three hornwort genomes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The total assembly length varied between 117 and 133 Mb, which is consistent with the size estimates based on <i>k</i>-mer analysis (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Tab1">1</a>) but slightly larger than those from flow cytometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Szövényi, P. et al. Establishment of Anthoceros agrestis as a model species for studying the biology of hornworts. BMC Plant Biol. 15, 98 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1726">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Bainard, J. D. &amp; Villarreal Aguilar, J. C. Genome size increases in recently diverged hornwort clades. Genome 56, 431–435 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1729">18</a></sup>. Although these genomes are among the smallest of land plants, their repetitive and transposable element contents are considerable (36–38%). Similar to other plant genomes, the most abundant repeats are long terminal repeat elements (&gt;20%) followed by a large number of unclassified repeats and DNA elements. The genome size variation among the three strains can be largely attributed to the differences in repeat content (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">4</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">4</a>). A combination of ab initio, evidence-based and comparative gene prediction approaches resulted in 24,700–25,800 predicted protein-coding genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">5</a>). For <i>A. agrestis</i> we also created a pan genome combining genome assemblies and gene annotations of the two strains (Bonn and Oxford) in a non-redundant way (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Sec14">Methods</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">Supplementary notes</a>). The three hornwort genomes show a high gene density compared to other land plants (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">6</a>). All three genomes and their annotations can be accessed, browsed, searched and downloaded from ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hornworts (Anthocerotophyta). University of Zurich &#xA;https://www.hornworts.uzh.ch/en.html&#xA;&#xA; (2020)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1759">19</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec3" data-title="Anthoceros displays unusual centromere structure"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec3"><i>Anthoceros</i> displays unusual centromere structure</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec3-content"><p>The chromosomal-level assembly of <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn revealed some peculiarities in the hornwort genome structures. In particular, we could not locate the typical vascular plant centromeric regions, which are usually composed of highly duplicated tandem repeats of 100–1,000 base pairs (bp)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Jiang, J., Birchler, J. A., Parrott, W. A. &amp; Dawe, R. K. A molecular view of plant centromeres. Trends Plant Sci. 8, 570–575 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1777">20</a></sup>. In <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn, tandem repeats with a unit size over 30 bp gave rise to only very short arrays, and these repeats do not show a clear spatial clustering (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">5</a>). While gene density does fluctuate along the scaffolds, extensive regions with low gene density typical for centromeric regions of vascular plants were missing. Similarly, we could not identify stretches of scaffolds having an elevated repeat content (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">4</a>), other than the putative telomeric regions. In other words, hornwort centromeres may not be characterized by a higher repeat density compared to other parts of the genome (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>). Similar genome organizations were also discovered in the <i>P. patens</i> genome where genes and repeats are evenly distributed along the chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Lang, D. et al. The Physcomitrella patens chromosome-scale assembly reveals moss genome structure and evolution. Plant J. 93, 515–533 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1800">21</a></sup>. While it is tempting to suggest that this genomic organization may be a shared feature of bryophyte genomes, we nevertheless cannot rule out the possibility that the bona fide centromeres were not sequenced or assembled properly despite the long-read and Hi-C data. Future work using immunolabelling is necessary to confirm this suggestion.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="Phylogenomic evidence for the monophyly of bryophytes"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">Phylogenomic evidence for the monophyly of bryophytes</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><p>To investigate the phylogenetic position of hornworts, we compiled 742 mostly single-copy genes from 21 genomes spanning major lineages of land plants and streptophyte algae. Monophyly of bryophytes is maximally supported in all our analyses, regardless of the data types (nucleotide or amino acid), tree inference methods (concatenation- or coalescent-based) and support measures (bootstrap or SH-aLRT or local posterior probability) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig2">2</a>). In addition, over 50% of the gene-tree quartets are consistent with hornworts being a sister clade to liverworts and mosses (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig2">2</a>). Our results add to the growing evidence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nishiyama, T. et al. Chloroplast phylogeny indicates that bryophytes are monophyletic. Mol. Biol. Evol. 21, 1813–1819 (2004)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1818">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wickett, N. J. et al. Phylotranscriptomic analysis of the origin and early diversification of land plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4859–E4868 (2014)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1818_1">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Puttick, M. N. et al. The interrelationships of land plants and the nature of the ancestral embryophyte. Curr. Biol. 28, 733–745 (2018)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1818_2">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="de Sousa, F., Foster, P. G., Donoghue, P. C. J., Schneider, H. &amp; Cox, C. J. Nuclear protein phylogenies support the monophyly of the three bryophyte groups (Bryophyta Schimp). New Phytol. 222, 565–575 (2019)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1818_3">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="One Thousand Plant Transcriptomes Initiative. One thousand plant transcriptomes and the phylogenomics of green plants. Nature 574, 679–685 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1821">6</a></sup> supporting two monophyletic groups of land plants: bryophytes and tracheophytes (vascular plants).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Land plant phylogeny inferred from 742 mostly single-copy genes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Land plant phylogeny inferred from 742 mostly single-copy genes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0618-2/MediaObjects/41477_2020_618_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0618-2/MediaObjects/41477_2020_618_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="357" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The monophyly of bryophytes is supported. The topology shown here is based on the maximum likelihood tree from the concatenated amino acid dataset. Thickened branches received maximal (100) bootstrap and SH-aLRT supports from both the concatenated nucleotide and amino acid datasets, as well as maximal posterior probabilities (1.0) from the Astral species-tree analysis (based on both nucleotide and amino acid gene trees). The inset shows the quartet frequencies among the 742 gene trees supporting monophyletic bryophytes (T1) versus two alternative placements of hornworts (T2 and T3). The dotted line shows the one-third threshold.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec5" data-title="Limited collinearity across bryophyte and vascular plant genomes"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec5-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec5">Limited collinearity across bryophyte and vascular plant genomes</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec5-content"><p>A previous study on the moss <i>P. patens</i> genome implied that regions showing collinearity between moss and some angiosperms may represent conserved collinear blocks since the MRCA of land plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Lang, D. et al. The Physcomitrella patens chromosome-scale assembly reveals moss genome structure and evolution. Plant J. 93, 515–533 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1844">21</a></sup>. However, comparing bryophytes to vascular plants, shared ancestral gene blocks could not be identified, rather that the collinear regions with vascular plants were unique to each of the bryophyte genomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">6</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">7</a>). The most genomic blocks collinear with at least one other land plant were found in the moss, followed by the liverwort and hornwort genomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">6</a>). Within bryophytes, no collinear segment conserved across all three lineages was found, although there were genomic regions exclusively collinear between each of two bryophyte genomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">6</a>). In general, there was more collinearity between the liverwort and the moss than between the hornwort and the liverwort/moss genomes. The numbers of such collinear regions, however, were small compared to those detected across vascular plants (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">6</a>). Altogether, these findings imply that the deep divergence of the moss, hornwort and liverwort genomes may have led to limited collinearity among bryophytes, as well as between bryophytes and vascular plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Absence of large-scale genome duplication in Anthoceros&#xA;"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Absence of large-scale genome duplication in <i>Anthoceros</i>
</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>Whole-genome duplications (WGD) have played an important role in shaping plant evolution and possibly underlie several adaptive radiations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Landis, J. B. et al. Impact of whole-genome duplication events on diversification rates in angiosperms. Am. J. Bot. 105, 348–363 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1877">22</a></sup>. A previous study, based on the number of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (K<sub>S</sub>) divergence in transcriptomic datasets, suggested that hornworts may not have experienced any WGD event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Lang, D. et al. The Physcomitrella patens chromosome-scale assembly reveals moss genome structure and evolution. Plant J. 93, 515–533 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1883">21</a></sup>, similar to <i>M. polymorpha</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287–299 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1889">8</a></sup> and <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Banks, J. A. et al. The Selaginella genome identifies genetic changes associated with the evolution of vascular plants. Science 332, 960–963 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1896">23</a></sup>. Our Ks plots on the annotated <i>Anthoceros</i> genes similarly show no sign of WGD (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">7</a>). To further corroborate this, we investigated patterns of intragenomic synteny in the three hornwort genomes, as well as the published <i>M. polymorpha</i> and <i>P. patens</i> genomes for comparison. We found very little self-synteny in the hornwort genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig1">1b–d</a>), providing strong evidence for the lack of WGD in <i>Anthoceros</i>. The high proportion of the genomes that are not syntenic is comparable to that in <i>M. polymorpha</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig1">1e</a>). On the other hand, <i>P. patens</i> shows a clear 1:1 (and some 1:2) self-syntenic relationship (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig1">1f</a>), which is consistent with the earlier report and indicative of two rounds of WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Lang, D. et al. The Physcomitrella patens chromosome-scale assembly reveals moss genome structure and evolution. Plant J. 93, 515–533 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1931">21</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Small repertoire of TAPs"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Small repertoire of TAPs</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>We found that 2.4–2.6% of the proteomes of the three <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes were annotated as transcription-associated proteins (TAPs) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig3">3a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">8</a>). Compared to other land plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Wilhelmsson, P. K. I., Mühlich, C., Ullrich, K. K. &amp; Rensing, S. A. Comprehensive genome-wide classification reveals that many plant-specific transcription factors evolved in streptophyte algae. Genome Biol. Evol. 9, 3384–3397 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d57167e1952">24</a></sup>, this is on the very low end of the spectrum both in terms of proportion and absolute number. Furthermore, about two-thirds (56) of the hornwort TAP families were smaller in size than in <i>M. polymorpha</i>. Given such a minimal TAP repertoire, hornworts can serve as an excellent baseline model to study the evolution and diversification of transcriptional networks. Despite its streamlined nature, some TAPs were only found in hornworts and vascular plants but not in the other two bryophyte genomes, with the most notable example being <i>YABBY</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). Such TAPs probably evolved in the MRCA of land plants but were lost in the mosses and liverworts. We also detected TAP families that were present in all streptophytes but lost either in the hornwort genomes (for example, SRS transcription factor, TF) or in <i>M. polymorpha</i> (for example type I MADS-box TF). Altogether, our findings suggest a dynamic TAP family turnover in the early evolution of land plants with multiple independent losses in different bryophyte lineages.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="TAPs and sporophyte development."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: TAPs and sporophyte development.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0618-2/MediaObjects/41477_2020_618_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0618-2/MediaObjects/41477_2020_618_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="288" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, The <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes have the smallest TAP repertoire among land plants. <b>b</b>, Sporophytes (red arrowhead) and gametophytes (blue arrow) of <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn. <b>c</b>, Stomata of <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn. <b>d</b>, Gene expression profiles across different developmental stages in <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn (<i>n</i> = 12 biologically independent samples; two-sided test for differential expression, false-discovery rate ≤0.05 and log<sub>2</sub>-fold-change ≥2). wk, week; mo, month.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Genes related to sporophyte development"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Genes related to sporophyte development</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>While hornworts have a gametophyte-dominant life cycle like other bryophytes, their sporophyte generation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig3">3b</a>) shows several unique features<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ligrone, R., Duckett, J. G. &amp; Renzaglia, K. S. The origin of the sporophyte shoot in land plants: a bryological perspective. Ann. Bot. 110, 935–941 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2021">25</a></sup>. First, after fertilization, the zygote division in hornworts is longitudinal, whereas zygotes in all other land plants undergo transverse division. Second, the hornwort sporophyte maintains a basal sporophytic meristem producing cells that continuously differentiate into mature tissues towards the tip. A common origin of indeterminate sporophyte development in hornworts and vascular plant shoot apical meristem (SAM) has been hypothesized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ligrone, R., Duckett, J. G. &amp; Renzaglia, K. S. The origin of the sporophyte shoot in land plants: a bryological perspective. Ann. Bot. 110, 935–941 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2025">25</a></sup>. Lastly, hornwort sporophytes have stomata (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig3">3c</a>) similar to mosses and vascular plants, and the basic regulation may be shared across all stomatous lineages of land plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chater, C. C. C., Caine, R. S., Fleming, A. J. &amp; Gray, J. E. Origins and evolution of stomatal development. Plant Physiol. 174, 624–638 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2032">26</a></sup>. Nevertheless, firm evidence supporting the homology of meristems as well as stomata is scarce. Here, we found that multiple genes critical for flowering plant SAM and stomata function have homologues in the hornwort genomes and are preferentially expressed in the sporophyte phase.</p><p>Class 1 <i>Knotted1</i>-like homeobox (KNOX1) genes regulate sporophytic meristem activity in both <i>P. patens</i> and vascular plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Coudert, Y., Novák, O. &amp; Harrison, C. J. A KNOX-cytokinin regulatory module predates the origin of indeterminate vascular plants. Current Biology 29, 2743–2750 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2045">27</a></sup>, while Class 2 <i>Knotted1</i>-like homeobox (KNOX2) genes maintain sporophyte cell fate in <i>P. patens</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Sakakibara, K. et al. KNOX2 genes regulate the haploid-to-diploid morphological transition in land plants. Science 339, 1067–1070 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2054">28</a></sup>. Interestingly, the KNOX1 orthologue is lost in the <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes and only KNOX2 genes were found (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">8</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">9</a>). The KNOX2 orthologues showed strong sporophyte-specific expression (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig3">3d</a>), which implies that the involvement of KNOX2 in maintaining sporophytic cell fate may be conserved in all land plants. Heterodimerization of KNOX1/KNOX2 and BELL-LIKE HOMEOBOX proteins is a deeply conserved molecular mechanism that is required for the KNOX functions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Arun, A. et al. Convergent recruitment of TALE homeodomain life cycle regulators to direct sporophyte development in land plants and brown algae. eLife 8, e43101 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2074">29</a></sup>. We found that in <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn, a single <i>BELL</i> and a single <i>KNOX2</i> gene were specifically expressed in the sporophyte phase. Nevertheless, contrary to our expectations, the <i>BELL</i> gene was more strongly expressed in the early stages while the <i>KNOX2</i> gene in the later stages of sporophyte development (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig3">3d</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">9</a>). This suggests that hornwort sporophyte identity may not be determined by KNOX2 through interaction with BELL. Nevertheless, this hypothesis needs functional verification because partially overlapping expression of the <i>KNOX2</i> and <i>BELL</i> genes does not exclude the possibility of heterodimerization.</p><p><i>WUSCHEL-related homeobox 13</i> like (WOX13L) genes are involved in zygote development and stem cell formation in the moss <i>P. patens</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Sakakibara, K. et al. WOX13-like genes are required for reprogramming of leaf and protoplast cells into stem cells in the moss Physcomitrella patens. Development 141, 1660–1670 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2117">30</a></sup><i>. A. thaliana WOX13</i> promotes replum formation in the fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Romera-Branchat, M., Ripoll, J. J., Yanofsky, M. F. &amp; Pelaz, S. The WOX 13 homeobox gene promotes replum formation in the Arabidopsis thaliana fruit. Plant J. 73, 37–49 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2123">31</a></sup> and <i>WOX14</i> promotes vascular cell differentiation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Denis, E. et al. WOX14 promotes bioactive gibberellin synthesis and vascular cell differentiation in Arabidopsis. Plant J. 90, 560–572 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2130">32</a></sup>. The <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes have four WOX13L members (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">8</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">9</a>) and <i>WOX13La</i> is specifically expressed in sporophytes while <i>WOX13Lbcd</i> have expression at both gametophyte and sporophyte generations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig3">3d</a>) and may have diverse roles in stem cell maintenance and sporophyte development. The <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes also have a single <i>FLORICAULA</i>/<i>LEAFY</i> (<i>FLO</i>/<i>LFY</i>) gene (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">8</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">9</a>), which in <i>P. patens</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> controls zygote development and SAM maintenance, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Tanahashi, T., Sumikawa, N., Kato, M. &amp; Hasebe, M. Diversification of gene function: homologs of the floral regulator FLO/LFY control the first zygotic cell division in the moss Physcomitrella patens. Development 132, 1727–1736 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2188">33</a></sup>. In hornworts, <i>LFY</i> is predominantly expressed in the gametophyte stages (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig3">3d</a>) while in <i>P. patens</i> it is expressed both in the gametophyte and the sporophyte. It is possible that such differences may contribute to the unique developmental pattern of hornwort sporophytes.</p><p>Stomatal development in <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>P. patens</i> is regulated by a conserved genetic toolbox, including the basic helix–loop–helix (bHLH) transcription factors SMF (<i>SPCH</i>, <i>MUTE</i> and <i>FAMA</i>), ICE/SCREAMs (<i>SCRM</i>s), <i>EPIDERMAL PATTERNING FACTOR</i> (<i>EPF</i>), <i>ERECTA</i> and <i>TOO MANY MOUTHS</i> (<i>TMM</i>) genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Lee, L. R. &amp; Bergmann, D. C. The plant stomatal lineage at a glance. J. Cell Sci. 132, jcs228551 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2240">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Chater, C. C. et al. Origin and function of stomata in the moss Physcomitrella patens. Nat. Plants 2, 16179 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2243">35</a></sup>. <i>FAMA</i> in particular is involved in the final guard cell differentiation step and serves as the key switch. Orthologues of <i>SMF</i>, <i>TMM</i> and <i>EPF</i> were absent in <i>M. polymorpha</i>, consistent with the fact that liverworts do not have stomata<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287–299 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2263">8</a></sup>. We found orthologues of <i>FAMA (SMF)</i>, <i>SCRM</i>, <i>ERECTA</i>, <i>EPF</i> and <i>TMM</i> in the <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes (in line with a previous study based on our earlier genome draft<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chater, C. C. C., Caine, R. S., Fleming, A. J. &amp; Gray, J. E. Origins and evolution of stomatal development. Plant Physiol. 174, 624–638 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2286">26</a></sup>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">10</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">9</a>). <i>SMF</i>, <i>SCRM</i>, <i>TMM</i> and <i>EPF</i> showed sporophyte-specific expression patterns (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig3">3d</a>), suggesting that they may have similar roles in stomatal patterning in hornworts. While <i>ERECTA</i> was also expressed during early sporophyte development, its expression fluctuated between replicates and results were inconclusive. <i>EPF</i> expression showed similar inconsistency among replicates but did not influence our conclusion about its sporophyte-specific expression. In addition to <i>EPF</i>, an <i>EPF</i>-like gene in the EPFL4-6 clade, was found in hornworts (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">9</a>), and is specifically expressed in gametophytes with a higher expression toward maturity and thus perhaps involved in a different cell–cell signalling other than stomatal regulation. <i>EPF4</i> and <i>EPF6</i> in <i>A. thaliana</i> are involved in coordination of the central and peripheral zone in SAM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Kosentka, P. Z., Overholt, A., Maradiaga, R., Mitoubsi, O. &amp; Shpak, E. D. EPFL signals in the boundary region of the SAM restrict its size and promote leaf initiation. Plant Physiol. 179, 265–279 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2337">36</a></sup>. Taken together, our data are consistent with a single origin of stomatal differentiation mechanism among all stomatous land plants, though positional determination may have evolved differently (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Genes related to phytohormone synthesis and signalling"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Genes related to phytohormone synthesis and signalling</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>The <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes contain the genetic chassis for the biosynthesis and signalling of abscisic acid, auxin, cytokinin, ethylene and jasmonate (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">10</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">12</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">10</a>), reaffirming the origins of these pathways in the MRCA of land plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Rensing, S. A. et al. The Physcomitrella genome reveals evolutionary insights into the conquest of land by plants. Science 319, 64–69 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2369">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287–299 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2372">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Nishiyama, T. et al. The Chara genome: secondary complexity and implications for plant terrestrialization. Cell 174, 448–464 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2375">37</a></sup>. Similar to <i>M. polymorpha</i> and <i>P. patens</i>, salicylic acid signalling components, but not the receptor-related genes, are found in hornworts. While <i>DELLA</i> is present, orthologues of gibberellin (GA) receptor GID1 and GA oxidases are missing from the <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes. This is consistent with the recent suggestion that DELLA was recruited to the GA signalling pathway later in plant evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Hernandez-Garcia, J. &amp; Briones-Moreno, A. Origin of gibberellin-dependent transcriptional regulation by molecular exploitation of a transactivation domain in DELLA proteins. Mol. Biol. Evol. 36, 908–918 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2391">38</a></sup>. Hornworts also possess enzymes to synthesize strigolactones but genes involved in strigolactone signalling are absent. This supports the idea that strigolactones are an ancient non-hormonal signal for rhizospheric communication with mycorrhizal fungi<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Walker, C. H., Siu-Ting, K., Taylor, A., O’Connell, M. J. &amp; Bennett, T. Strigolactone synthesis is ancestral in land plants, but canonical strigolactone signalling is a flowering plant innovation. BMC Biol. 17, 70 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2396">39</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Genetic network for arbuscular mycorrhizal symbiosis was present in the MRCA of land plants"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Genetic network for arbuscular mycorrhizal symbiosis was present in the MRCA of land plants</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>The symbiotic relationship with arbuscular mycorrhizal fungi (AMF) is one of the key innovations underlying the successful colonization and diversification on land of plants. Evidence of AMF can be found inside plant megafossils 407 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Remy, W., Taylor, T. N., Hass, H. &amp; Kerp, H. Four hundred-million-year-old vesicular arbuscular mycorrhizae. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 91, 11841–11843 (1994)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2408">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Strullu-Derrien, C. Fossil filamentous microorganisms associated with plants in early terrestrial environments. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 44, 122–128 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2411">41</a></sup> and in almost all extant plant lineages (hornworts, liverworts and vascular plants). Recent genetic studies have identified a suite of genes in the angiosperms that regulate the establishment and maintenance of AMF symbiosis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="MacLean, A. M., Bravo, A. &amp; Harrison, M. J. Plant signaling and metabolic pathways enabling arbuscular mycorrhizal symbiosis. Plant Cell 29, 2319–2335 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2415">42</a></sup>. Some of these genes are also required for legume–rhizobial interaction and are often referred to as the common symbiosis genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Parniske, M. Arbuscular mycorrhiza: the mother of plant root endosymbioses. Nat. Rev. Microbiol. 6, 763–775 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2419">43</a></sup>.</p><p>While a few components can be traced back to as far as charophyte algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Delaux, P.-M. et al. Algal ancestor of land plants was preadapted for symbiosis. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, 13390–13395 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2426">44</a></sup>, the question of when exactly did the entire AMF symbiosis genetic network originate remains open. This is partly because both the bryophytes that have published genomes to date (<i>P. patens</i> and <i>M. polymorpha</i>) are incapable of AMF symbiosis and may have secondarily lost the symbiosis genes, as exemplified in some angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Delaux, P.-M. et al. Comparative phylogenomics uncovers the impact of symbiotic associations on host genome evolution. PLoS Genet. 10, e1004487 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2436">45</a></sup>. Here, we show that all the key angiosperm AMF symbiosis genes have orthologues in the three hornwort genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig4">4</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">11</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">13</a>). Although their roles in hornwort AMF symbiosis remain to be tested, this result provides strong evidence that the genetic infrastructure required for AMF symbiosis was already present in the MRCA of land plants. Importantly, the presence of these genes in liverworts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Delaux, P.-M. et al. Algal ancestor of land plants was preadapted for symbiosis. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, 13390–13395 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2450">44</a></sup> and hornworts makes this conclusion insensitive to any uncertainty of the land plant phylogeny. We have not succeeded in reconstituting hornwort–AMF symbiosis in vitro and hence are unable to test expression of these orthologues in the context of AMF. Nevertheless, we found that in both <i>A. agrestis</i> (Oxford strain) and <i>A. punctatus</i>, one of the AMF symbiosis genes, <i>RAM1</i>, was upregulated when plants were nitrogen-starved (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig4">4</a>). Nitrogen limitation is a major trigger for cyanobacteria symbiosis in hornworts, which might implicate the involvement of RAM1 in symbiosis, but further genetic studies are needed.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Evolution and genetics of symbiosis in hornworts."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Evolution and genetics of symbiosis in hornworts.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0618-2/MediaObjects/41477_2020_618_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0618-2/MediaObjects/41477_2020_618_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="850" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Orthologues of AMF symbiosis pathway genes can be found in hornworts, indicating their presence in the common ancestor of land plants. The asterisk indicates that the <i>M. paleacea</i> transcriptome was searched instead of <i>M. polymorpha</i> genome because the latter secondarily lost AMF. <b>b</b>, RAM1 is upregulated during nitrogen starvation in both <i>A. agrestis</i> and <i>A. punctatus</i>. <b>c</b>, Reconstituted <i>Anthoceros</i>–cyanobacteria symbiosis. Arrowhead points to a cyanobacteria colony. <b>d</b>, Transcriptomic responses to nitrogen starvation and cyanobacterial symbiosis in <i>A. agrestis</i> (<i>n</i> = 9 biologically independent samples). PC1 and PC2 refer to the first and second axes of principal component analysis on gene expression values. <b>e</b>, A suite of genes were highly upregulated under symbiosis in both <i>A. agrestis</i> and <i>A. punctatus</i> (two-sided test for differential expression, false-discovery rate ≤0.05 and log<sub>2</sub>-fold-change &gt;4).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Genes related to cyanobacterial symbiosis"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Genes related to cyanobacterial symbiosis</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>Symbiosis with nitrogen-fixing cyanobacteria is a rare trait, with limited appearances in a few plant lineages: bryophytes, <i>Azolla</i> (ferns), cycads (gymnosperms) and <i>Gunnera</i> (angiosperms)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Meeks, J. C. Symbiosis between nitrogen-fixing cyanobacteria and plants. Bioscience 48, 266–276 (1998)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2535">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Adams, D. G. &amp; Duggan, P. S. Cyanobacteria–bryophyte symbioses. J. Exp. Bot. 59, 1047–1058 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2538">46</a></sup>. In bryophytes, although mosses frequently harbour epiphytic cyanobacteria<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Rousk, K., Jones, D. L. &amp; DeLuca, T. H. Moss–cyanobacteria associations as biogenic sources of nitrogen in boreal forest ecosystems. Front. Microbiol. 4, 150 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2542">47</a></sup>, only hornworts and two liverwort species host cyanobacteria endophytically within specialized slime-filled cavities<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Meeks, J. C. Symbiosis between nitrogen-fixing cyanobacteria and plants. Bioscience 48, 266–276 (1998)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2546">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Adams, D. G. &amp; Duggan, P. S. Cyanobacteria–bryophyte symbioses. J. Exp. Bot. 59, 1047–1058 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2549">46</a></sup>. Amongst all the plant associations with cyanobacteria, most of the research has been done on hornworts, using <i>A. punctatus</i> (sequenced here) and the cyanobacterium <i>Nostoc punctiforme</i> as the study system.</p><p>Although several cyanobacterial genes from <i>N. punctiforme</i> have been identified that are key to initiation of symbiotic association<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Meeks, J. C. Physiological adaptations in nitrogen-fixing Nostoc–plant symbiotic associations. Microbiol. Monogr. 8, 181–205 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2566">16</a></sup>, nothing is known about the hornwort genetics. Here we generated RNA-seq data to compare the gene expression of symbiont-free (either nitrogen-starved or nitrogen-fed) and symbiosis-reconstituted hornworts (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig4">4</a>). This experiment was conducted with both <i>A. punctatus</i> and the <i>A. agrestis</i> Oxford isolate. We identified 40 genes that, when the cyanobionts are present, are highly induced (&gt;16-fold) in both hornwort species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig4">4</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">12</a>). These include a number of receptor kinases, transcription factors and transporters. Of particular interest is a SWEET sugar transporter in the <i>SWEET16/17</i> clade (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig4">4</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">14</a>), which is minimally transcribed under the symbiont-free states but is among the highest expressed genes in symbiosis (&gt;10<sup>3</sup> fold-change). The upregulation of <i>SWEET</i> in symbiosis is interesting because it implies that this sugar transporter is dedicated to supplying carbon rewards to the cyanobionts. This implication is supported by the fact that only exogenous glucose, fructose or sucrose sustained dark nitrogen fixation in the <i>A</i>. <i>punctatus</i>–<i>N</i>. <i>punctiforme</i> association<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Steinberg, N. A. &amp; Meeks, J. C. Physiological sources of reductant for nitrogen-fixation activity in Nostoc sp. strain UCD 7801 in symbiotic association with Anthoceros punctatus. J. Bacteriol. 173, 7324–7329 (1991)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2613">48</a></sup> and by the observation that inactivation of a carbohydrate permease in <i>N</i>. <i>punctiforme</i> resulted in a defective symbiotic phenotype<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Ekman, M., Picossi, S., Campbell, E. L., Meeks, J. C. &amp; Flores, E. A Nostoc punctiforme sugar transporter necessary to establish a cyanobacterium–plant symbiosis. Plant Physiol. 161, 1984–1992 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2624">49</a></sup>. In parallel, SWEET is involved in mycorrhizal symbiosis as well, but a different orthologue, in the <i>SWEET1</i> clade, was recruited<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="An, J. et al. A Medicago truncatula SWEET transporter implicated in arbuscule maintenance during arbuscular mycorrhizal symbiosis. New Phytol. 224, 396–408 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2631">50</a></sup>.</p><p>Another gene of interest is subtilase. Members of this gene family have been shown to be highly upregulated in a wide variety of microbial symbioses, including rhizobial<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kistner, C. et al. Seven Lotus japonicus genes required for transcriptional reprogramming of the root during fungal and bacterial symbiosis. Plant Cell 17, 2217–2229 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2638">51</a></sup>, mycorrhizal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Takeda, N., Sato, S., Asamizu, E., Tabata, S. &amp; Parniske, M. Apoplastic plant subtilases support arbuscular mycorrhiza development in Lotus japonicus. Plant J. 58, 766–777 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2642">52</a></sup> and actinorhizal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fournier, J. et al. Cell remodeling and subtilase gene expression in the actinorhizal plant Discaria trinervis highlight host orchestration of intercellular Frankia colonization. New Phytol. 219, 1018–1030 (2018)." href="#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2646">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ribeiro, A., Akkermans, A. D., van Kammen, A., Bisseling, T. &amp; Pawlowski, K. A nodule-specific gene encoding a subtilisin-like protease is expressed in early stages of actinorhizal nodule development. Plant Cell 7, 785–794 (1995)." href="#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2646_1">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Svistoonoff, S. et al. cg12 expression is specifically linked to infection of root hairs and cortical cells during Casuarina glauca and Allocasuarina verticillata actinorhizal nodule development. Mol. Plant Microbe Interact. 16, 600–607 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2649">55</a></sup> interactions. RNA interference knockdown of a subtilase (<i>SBTM1</i>) in the legume <i>Lotus japonicus</i> resulted in a decreased arbuscule formation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Takeda, N., Sato, S., Asamizu, E., Tabata, S. &amp; Parniske, M. Apoplastic plant subtilases support arbuscular mycorrhiza development in Lotus japonicus. Plant J. 58, 766–777 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2660">52</a></sup>. Here, we found that in both <i>A. punctatus</i> and <i>A. agrestis</i>, a subtilase homologue was similarly induced by cyanobacteria symbiosis.
