Phylogenetic reconstruction showed that this hornwort subtilase is not orthologous to those involved in other plant symbioses (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">15</a>). Taken together, our results imply that hornworts might have convergently recruited SWEET and subtilase for cyanobacterial symbiosis, although in both cases not the same orthologues were used as in other plant–microbe symbioses.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Pyrenoid-based CCM"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Pyrenoid-based CCM</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><p>To enable a more efficient photosynthesis, hornworts, cyanobacteria and many eukaryotic algae have evolved biophysical CCM inside their cells (cyanobacteria) or individual chloroplasts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Meyer, M. T., Whittaker, C. &amp; Griffiths, H. The algal pyrenoid: key unanswered questions. J. Exp. Bot. 68, 3739–3749 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2682">56</a></sup>. Algal and hornwort chloroplasts use inorganic carbon transporters and carbonic anhydrases to locally concentrate CO<sub>2</sub> in the pyrenoids, a specialized chloroplast compartment where RuBisCOs aggregates. Pyrenoids can thus boost photosynthetic efficiency and reduce photorespiration. Such pyrenoid-based CCM has been extensively studied in the model green alga <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i> with the hope of installing a CCM in crop plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Rae, B. D. et al. Progress and challenges of engineering a biophysical CO2-concentrating mechanism into higher plants. J. Exp. Bot. 68, 3717–3737 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2691">57</a></sup>.</p><p>Hornworts are the only land plants with a pyrenoid-based CCM. Interestingly, for the past 100 million years, pyrenoids in hornworts are inferred to have been repeatedly lost and gained<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Villarreal Aguilar, J. C. &amp; Renner, S. S. Hornwort pyrenoids, carbon-concentrating structures, evolved and were lost at least five times during the last 100 million years. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 18873–18878 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2698">58</a></sup>, suggesting that pyrenoid development and function is controlled by a few master switches. The genetics behind hornwort pyrenoids, however, has remained completely unknown. We explored whether hornwort genomes have genes that are known to be required for pyrenoid-based CCM in <i>C. reinhardtii</i>. While many of the <i>C. reinhardtii</i> CCM genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Rae, B. D. et al. Progress and challenges of engineering a biophysical CO2-concentrating mechanism into higher plants. J. Exp. Bot. 68, 3717–3737 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2708">57</a></sup> do not have clear homologues in hornworts (nor in any other land plants), we did find <i>LCIB</i> (low CO<sub>2</sub> inducible B) to be present in the hornwort genomes and hornwort transcriptomes of the 1,000 plant transcriptomes project (1KP)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="One Thousand Plant Transcriptomes Initiative. One thousand plant transcriptomes and the phylogenomics of green plants. Nature 574, 679–685 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2718">6</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig5">5</a>). Apart from hornworts, no <i>LCIB</i> homologue could be found in other plant genomes sequenced to date. The uniquely shared presence of LCIB in pyrenoid-bearing algae and hornworts implies that <i>LCIB</i> might have a role in the hornwort CCM. The phylogenetic tree indicates that the hornwort <i>LCIB</i>s form a sister clade to the <i>Klebsormidium nitens</i> homologue (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig5">5</a>) and thus is consistent with the organisms tree with many losses in various lineages. In this scenario, the MRCA of land plants had LCIB.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Relationship between LCIB and pyrenoid-based CCM."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Relationship between <i>LCIB</i> and pyrenoid-based CCM.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0618-2/MediaObjects/41477_2020_618_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0618-2/MediaObjects/41477_2020_618_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="556" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Phylogeny of <i>LCIB</i>. Numbers above branches are bootstrap support values (branches thickened when bootstrap &gt;70). <b>b</b>, Hornwort <i>LCIB</i>s have conserved amino acid residues at the active site. Yellow and green arrowheads point to the zinc-binding and catalytic residues, respectively. K.n., <i>K. nitens</i>; C.r., <i>C. reinhardtii</i>; O.l., <i>Ostreococcus lucimarinus</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In <i>C. reinhardtii</i>, <i>LCIB</i> gene expression is highly induced by CO<sub>2</sub> limitation and the encoded proteins localize around pyrenoids to presumably block CO<sub>2</sub> leakage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Wang, Y. &amp; Spalding, M. H. LCIB in the Chlamydomonas CO2-concentrating mechanism. Photosyn. Res. 121, 185–192 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2788">59</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Atkinson, N. et al. Introducing an algal carbon‐concentrating mechanism into higher plants: location and incorporation of key components. Plant Biotechnol. J. 14, 1302–1315 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2791">60</a></sup>. All the hornwort LCIB sequences have the conserved amino acid residues at the active sites that are shared with other algal LCIBs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Jin, S. et al. Structural insights into the LCIB protein family reveals a new group of β-carbonic anhydrases. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 14716–14721 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2796">61</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig5">5b</a>). However, unlike <i>C. reinhardtii</i>, we did not find <i>LCIB</i> to be differentially expressed when plants are grown at different CO<sub>2</sub> levels (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">12</a>). This, nevertheless, cannot rule out the involvement of <i>LCIB</i> in CCM because hornwort CCM was reported to be constitutively expressed and not regulated by CO<sub>2</sub> level<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Hanson, D. T., Renzaglia, K. &amp; Villarreal, J. C. in Photosynthesis in Bryophytes and Early Land Plants (eds Hanson, D. T. &amp; Rice, S. K.) 95–111 (Springer, 2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2820">62</a></sup>. Whether <i>LCIB</i> homologues have a similar function and localization in hornworts remains to be experimentally tested.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><p>The hornwort genomes presented here offer a unique window into the biology of land plant MRCA. For example, the <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes lack KNOX1, while <i>P. patens</i> and <i>M. polymorpha</i> lack <i>YABBY</i> genes. This suggests that the MRCA of land plants had both of these key developmental genes and independent gene losses occurred in different bryophyte lineages. While <i>LEAFY</i> expression is predominantly in the gametophyte stage, <i>YABBY</i>, <i>KNOX2</i>, <i>BELL</i> and some <i>WOX13L</i> genes are up-regulated in the hornwort sporophytes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig3">3</a>). In addition, several stomata-related genes are present in the <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes and expressed in early sporophyte development (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig3">3</a>), implying a homology of stomata at the genetic level. Finally, we found that the genes required for AMF symbiosis are conserved in <i>Anthoceros</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig4">4</a>), providing evidence that the MRCA of land plants was already equipped with the genetic network for AMF symbiosis. In-depth analysis on the evolution of the plant hormones (abscisic acid, auxin, gibberellin, jasmonate, salicylic acid and strigolactone), light signalling, peptidoglycan synthesis and chloroplast development can be found in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>.</p><p>The <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes shared several features with the two other published bryophyte genomes. Most notable is the absence of tandem repeats that make up the typical centromeric regions. Further studies are needed to identify the centromeric regions and understand their structure. While <i>P. patens</i> has experienced two rounds of WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Lang, D. et al. The Physcomitrella patens chromosome-scale assembly reveals moss genome structure and evolution. Plant J. 93, 515–533 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2891">21</a></sup>, none can be found in <i>Anthoceros</i> and <i>M. polymorpha</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig1">1</a>). This might explain the minimal representation of transcription factors in the last two genomes.</p><p>Furthermore, our functional genomic data shed light on the genetic framework that underpins features that are unique to hornworts. We identified a suite of candidate genes underlying hornwort–cyanobacteria symbiosis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig4">4</a>). This includes a SWEET transporter that might be involved in nutrient transfer with the cyanobionts. A well-characterized <i>C. reinhardtii</i> CCM gene, <i>LCIB</i>, was conserved in hornworts but apparently lost in all other plant lineages (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig5">5</a>). Whether <i>LCIB</i> also participates in hornwort CCM awaits future functional characterization.</p><p>The recent advances of ‘seed-free genomics’ have greatly improved our understanding of streptophyte evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287–299 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2926">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Lang, D. et al. The Physcomitrella patens chromosome-scale assembly reveals moss genome structure and evolution. Plant J. 93, 515–533 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2929">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Banks, J. A. et al. The Selaginella genome identifies genetic changes associated with the evolution of vascular plants. Science 332, 960–963 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2932">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Nishiyama, T. et al. The Chara genome: secondary complexity and implications for plant terrestrialization. Cell 174, 448–464 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2935">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, F.-W. et al. Fern genomes elucidate land plant evolution and cyanobacterial symbioses. Nat. Plants 4, 460–472 (2018)." href="#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2938">63</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hori, K. et al. Klebsormidium flaccidum genome reveals primary factors for plant terrestrial adaptation. Nat. Commun. 5, 3978 (2014)." href="#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2938_1">64</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cheng, S. et al. Genomes of subaerial Zygnematophyceae provide insights into land plant evolution. Cell 179, 1057–1067 (2019)." href="#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2938_2">65</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Extreme haplotype variation in the desiccation-tolerant clubmoss Selaginella lepidophylla. Nat. Commun. 9, 13 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2941">66</a></sup>. Here, our hornwort genomes fill in yet another critical gap and are beginning to illuminate the dawn of land plants as well as the unique biology of hornworts.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Plant materials</h3><p>Cultures of <i>A. agrestis</i> (Oxford and Bonn strains) and <i>A. punctatus</i> were all derived from a single spore and axenically propagated and maintained on either BCD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Cove, D. J. et al. Culturing the moss Physcomitrella patens. Cold Spring Harb. Protoc. 2009, db.prot5136 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2963">67</a></sup> or Hatcher’s<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Hatcher, R. E. Towards the establishment of a pure culture collection of Hepaticae. Bryologist 68, 227–231 (1965)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2967">68</a></sup> media. Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">13</a> shows the origin and specimen voucher for each of the three strains.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Chromosome count</h3><p>The tip of an <i>A. agrestis</i> Oxford gametophyte thallus was cut into small pieces and fixed with 4% glutaraldehyde in 0.05 M phosphate buffer (pH 7.0) for 12 h at 4 °C. After washing with the buffer for 10 min, cell walls were digested for 2 h with a solution containing 1% Driselase (Sigma-Aldrich), 1% Cellulase Onozuka RS (Yakult), 1% Pectolyase (Kikkoman), 0.5% IGEPAL CA-630 and 1% bovine serum albumin (BSA) at 30 °C. After several washes with the buffer, the samples were incubated in 0.05 M phosphate buffer containing 0.1% TritonX-100 for 12 h at 4 °C. After several washes with the buffer, the samples were transferred onto MAS coated slide glasses (Matsunami Glass) and coverslipped. The slides were then pressed with a thumb directly over the coverslip. After removal of the coverslip, the slides were air-dried for 10 min at room temperature and then extracted with methanol at −20 °C for 10 min. After the staining with the buffer containing 1 μg l 4,6-diamidino-2-phenylindole (DAPI) for 5 min, the slides were mounted with Vectashield mounting medium (Vector Laboratory Burlingame) and observed with a fluorescence microscope under ultraviolet-light excitation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">DNA sequencing</h3><p>Hornwort DNA was extracted using a CTAB-precipitation method modified from ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Nagar, R. &amp; Schwessinger, B. High purity, high molecular weight DNA extraction from rust spores via CTAB based DNA precipitation for long read sequencing v1. protocols.io &#xA;https://doi.org/10.17504/protocols.io.n5ydg7w&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d57167e2993">69</a></sup>. Nanopore libraries were prepared by SQK-LSK108 and sequenced on MinION R9 flow cells for 48 h. Basecalling was done by Albacore.</p><p>For <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn, the TrueSeq DNA Nano Kit (Illumina) was used to prepare paired-end (PE) sequencing libraries which were sequenced (PE 150 bp) on HiSeq4000 at the Functional Genomic Center Zurich (FGCZ). For <i>A. agrestis</i> Oxford 251 PE reads, a PCR-Free library was prepared using a KAPA Hyper Prep Kit according to the protocol published by Broad Institute<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Weisenfeld, N. I. et al. Comprehensive variation discovery in single human genomes. Nat. Genet. 46, 1350–1355 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3006">70</a></sup>). The library was mixed (5%) with other barcoded libraries and sequenced on Illumina HiSeq1500 (two lanes with Rapid mode; OnBoardClustering) at the National Institute of Basic Biology. For <i>A. punctatus</i>, Illumina genomic libraries were prepared by BGI and sequenced on HiSeq4000. Read quality and adaptor trimming was done by fastp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Chen, S., Zhou, Y., Chen, Y. &amp; Gu, J. fastp: an ultra-fast all-in-one FASTQ preprocessor. Bioinformatics 34, i884–i890 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3013">71</a></sup> with the default setting. For <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn, additional Chicago and Hi-C libraries were prepared by DoveTail Genomics. A total of two Chicago libraries and one Hi-C library were prepared with a physical coverage of 300× and 200×.</p><p>To calculate the read mapping rates, trimmed reads were mapped to the final assemblies using bwa mem -M<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3024">72</a></sup> and sorted with samtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3028">73</a></sup>. The mean insert size and #READ_PAIRS were calculated using picard CollectInsertSizeMetrics. Unmapped reads were counted with samtools view -c -f 4 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3032">73</a></sup>) and divided with the total number of reads to calculate percentage mapped. High-quality mapped reads were counted with -q 20. Reads mapped to chloroplast and mitochondrial genomes were counted with samtools. The bam files were assessed with qualimap v.2.2.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Okonechnikov, K., Conesa, A. &amp; García-Alcalde, F. Qualimap 2: advanced multi-sample quality control for high-throughput sequencing data. Bioinformatics 32, 292–294 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3036">74</a></sup>) bamqc and observed error rates (total, mismatch, insertions and deletions), as well as the genome coverage were recovered.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genome assembly</h3><p>Genome sizes for the three <i>Anthoceros</i> were estimated based on <i>k</i>-mer distribution by Jellyfish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Marçais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764–770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3054">75</a></sup> in conjunction with GenomeScope<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Vurture, G. W. et al. GenomeScope: fast reference-free genome profiling from short reads. Bioinformatics 33, 2202–2204 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3058">76</a></sup>. Draft assembly for <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn strain was first generated using a hybrid approach including Oxford nanopore (~60×) and Illumina paired-end reads (~150×) using MaSuRCA v.3.2.8 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Zimin, A. V. et al. The MaSuRCA genome assembler. Bioinformatics 29, 2669–2677 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3066">77</a></sup>). After assembly, base call quality was improved by two rounds of Pilon polishing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3070">78</a></sup>. We mapped Chicago and Hi-C reads back to the draft assembly and used DoveTail’s HiRise assembler v.2.1.2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Putnam, N. H. et al. Chromosome-scale shotgun assembly using an in vitro method for long-range linkage. Genome Res. 26, 342–350 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3074">79</a></sup>) for scaffolding. Contigs of the draft assembly were first scaffolded using the Chicago library to correct smaller scale errors and improve contiguity. Finally, the output assembly was further scaffolded using the Hi-C libraries and DoveTail’s HiRise assembler v.2.1.2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Putnam, N. H. et al. Chromosome-scale shotgun assembly using an in vitro method for long-range linkage. Genome Res. 26, 342–350 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3078">79</a></sup>) to derive the final assembly.</p><p>Genome assemblies of <i>A. agrestis</i> Oxford strain and <i>A. punctatus</i> were generated with the minimap2-miniasm assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Li, H. Minimap and miniasm: fast mapping and de novo assembly for noisy long sequences. Bioinformatics 32, 2103–2110 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3091">80</a></sup> using only the nanopore reads. We then used four iterations of minimap2-racon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Vaser, R., Sović, I., Nagarajan, N. &amp; Šikić, M. Fast and accurate de novo genome assembly from long uncorrected reads. Genome Res. 27, 737–746 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3095">81</a></sup> to derive the consensus sequence, followed by six rounds of Pilon polishing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3099">78</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Contamination removal</h3><p>While our cultures were grown in a putative axenic condition, low level of contamination cannot be completely ruled out. We therefore used blobtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Laetsch, D. R. &amp; Blaxter, M. L. BlobTools: interrogation of genome assemblies. F1000Res. 6, 1287 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3112">82</a></sup> to identify scaffolds/contigs primarily consisting of contaminant sequences. The Hi-C library theoretically should sort DNA sequences originating from different organisms because cross-linking occurred within the nuclei. Therefore, we hypothesized that dropping scaffolds mainly with non-streptophyte affiliation will effectively remove contaminants from our assembly. For <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn, we used both the full uniprot and the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) nucleotide database and blobtools to assign the taxonomic affiliation to each scaffold with an <i>e</i>-value of 10<sup>−4</sup>. We found that some of the small scaffolds were classified as of non-streptophyte origin with high confidence; these scaffolds were then removed from the assembly. For the <i>A. agrestis</i> Oxford and <i>A. punctatus</i> genomes, assemblies were contamination-filtered in a similar way. The detailed summary can be found in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">14</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">RNA-seq dataset and analysis on developmental stages</h3><p>To study the expression pattern of transcription factor genes across developmental stages, we generated RNA-seq libraries for the following stages of the <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn strain in two biological replicates: (1) spores after 2 weeks of germination, (2) 4-week-old gametophytes, (3) 2-month-old gametophytes, (4) sporophytes shorter than 5 mm, (5) sporophytes of 5–10 mm, (6) sporophytes longer than 1 cm with brown or black tips. Plants were grown on agar plates containing BCD medium<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Cove, D. J. et al. Culturing the moss Physcomitrella patens. Cold Spring Harb. Protoc. 2009, db.prot5136 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3145">67</a></sup> at 22 °C. RNA was extracted with the Spectrum Total RNA Plant Kit (Sigma-Aldrich) and stranded RNA-seq libraries were prepared using the TrueSeq Stranded mRNA Library Prep Kit (Illumina). Libraries were sequenced at the FGCZ on a HiSeq4000 machine. We used trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3149">83</a></sup> to quality filter and trim the raw reads. Gene expression was estimated using Salmon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Patro, R., Duggal, G., Love, M. I., Irizarry, R. A. &amp; Kingsford, C. Salmon provides fast and bias-aware quantification of transcript expression. Nat. Methods 14, 417–419 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3153">84</a></sup> and differential expression done by DESeq2 (log<sub>2</sub>-fold ≥2, false-discovery rate ≤0.05 and normalized reads counts)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Love, M. I., Huber, W. &amp; Anders, S. Moderated estimation of fold change and dispersion for RNA-seq data with DESeq2. Genome Biol. 15, 550 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3160">85</a></sup>.</p><p>We also generated separate thallus RNA-seq data for the Oxford strain (for annotation purpose). The plants were cultured on solid BCD plates and total RNA was extracted using RNeasy Plant Mini Kit (QIAGEN). The library was prepared using the TrueSeq stranded mRNA Library Prep Kit (Illumina) and sequenced on Hiseq1500.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">RNA-seq dataset and analysis on cyanobacterial symbiosis</h3><p>Liquid cultures of <i>A. agrestis</i> Oxford and <i>A. punctatus</i> were used in this experiment. To establish liquid cultures, plants were transferred from solid BCD plates to flasks with 100 ml of BCD media solution and placed on an orbital shaker at 130 r.p.m. for 2 weeks. For the cyano–/N+ and cyano–/N– conditions, plants were transferred to fresh new BCD solution with and without KNO<sub>3</sub>, respectively and grown for 10 d before harvest. To reconstitute cyanobacterial symbiosis (with <i>N. punctiforme</i> ATCC 29133), we followed the method of Enderlin and Meeks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Enderlin, C. S. &amp; Meeks, J. C. Pure culture and reconstitution of the Anthoceros–Nostoc symbiotic association. Planta 158, 157–165 (1983)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3186">86</a></sup> but using BCD as the growth medium. Three biological replicates were done for each condition. RNA was extracted by the Spectrum Total RNA Plant Kit (Sigma-Aldrich). The Illumina libraries were prepared by BGI and sequenced on HiSeq4000. Sequencing reads were mapped to the respective genomes by HiSat2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357–360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3191">87</a></sup>) and transcript abundance quantified by Stringtie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290–295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3195">88</a></sup>. We used DESeq2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Love, M. I., Huber, W. &amp; Anders, S. Moderated estimation of fold change and dispersion for RNA-seq data with DESeq2. Genome Biol. 15, 550 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3199">85</a></sup>) to carry out differential gene expression analysis, with false-discovery rate set to 0.005 and log<sub>2</sub>-fold-change threshold set to 1. To identify genes that are differentially expressed in both <i>A. agrestis</i> Oxford and <i>A. punctatus</i>, we used the Orthofinder gene family classification results (see below) coupled with phylogenetic analysis if needed.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">RNA-seq dataset and analysis on CO<sub>2</sub> response</h3><p>For the CO<sub>2</sub> experiment, we grew hornworts in magenta boxes with vented lids to allow air circulation while maintaining sterility. <i>A. agrestis</i> Oxford strain was used in this experiment and kept on solid BCD medium. We subjected the plant cultures to one of the three CO<sub>2</sub> environments at 150 ppm (low), 400 ppm (ambient) and 800 ppm (high) in a CO<sub>2</sub>-controlled growth chamber for 10 d (12 h/12 h day/night cycle). Three biological replicates were done for each treatment. RNA was extracted by the Spectrum Total RNA Plant Kit (Sigma-Aldrich). The Illumina libraries were prepared by BGI and sequenced on HiSeq4000. One of the low CO<sub>2</sub> samples failed to produce high-quality library, and as a result the low CO<sub>2</sub> condition has only two replicates. RNA-seq data analysis was done following the same procedure as described above. We used BiNGO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Maere, S., Heymans, K. &amp; Kuiper, M. BiNGO: a Cytoscape plugin to assess overrepresentation of gene ontology categories in biological networks. Bioinformatics 21, 3448–3449 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3236">89</a></sup> for gene ontology enrichment analysis and REVIGO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Supek, F., Bošnjak, M., Škunca, N. &amp; Šmuc, T. REVIGO summarizes and visualizes long lists of gene ontology terms. PLoS ONE 6, e21800 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3240">90</a></sup> to summarize and visualize the results.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Repeat annotation</h3><p>For repeat annotation, we first built custom repeat libraries for each genome using RepeatModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Smit, A. F. A. &amp; Hubley, R. RepeatModeler Open-1.0 (Institute for Systems Biology, accessed February 2019); &#xA;www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;&#xA;&#xA;" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3252">91</a></sup> and LTR_retriever<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of long terminal repeat retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 176, 1410–1422 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3256">92</a></sup>. The libraries were filtered to remove protein-coding genes by blasting against the UniProt plant database. We then used RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Smit, A. F. A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker Open-4.0 (Institute for Systems Biology, accessed February 2019); &#xA;www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;&#xA;&#xA;" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3260">93</a></sup> to annotate and mask the repetitive regions for each genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">RNA-seq, transcript and protein evidence</h3><p>We pooled <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn, Oxford and <i>A. punctatus</i> RNA-seq reads together and mapped them onto each of the genome assemblies using HiSat2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357–360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3278">87</a></sup>). We used all RNA-seq evidence available owing to the low nucleotide divergence among the three genomes. Transcriptomes were assembled for each species/strain separately. We used Portcullis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Mapleson, D., Venturini, L., Kaithakottil, G. &amp; Swarbreck, D. Efficient and accurate detection of splice junctions from RNA-seq with Portcullis. Gigascience 7, giy131 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3282">94</a></sup> to filter out bad splice junctions and Stringtie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290–295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3286">88</a></sup> to assemble the transcripts. We additionally used Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3291">95</a></sup> to generate both de novo and genome-guided transcriptome assemblies. We combined Trinity transcripts using the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments (PASA) pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3295">96</a></sup> and derived high-quality transcripts with Mikado<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Venturini, L., Caim, S., Kaithakottil, G. G., Mapleson, D. L. &amp; Swarbreck, D. Leveraging multiple transcriptome assembly methods for improved gene structure annotation. Gigascience 7, giy093 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3299">97</a></sup>. To obtain protein homology information, we retrieved the 19 proteomes (only primary transcripts; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">15</a>) and aligned them to the genome assemblies using exonerate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Slater, G. S. C. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinf. 6, 31 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3306">98</a></sup>. We kept only hits with at least 60% coverage and a similarity above 60%.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Gene prediction</h3><p>We used RNA-seq, transcript and protein evidence to train Augustus (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene prediction in eukaryotes that allows user-defined constraints. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W465–W467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3319">99</a></sup>) within Braker2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Hoff, K. J., Lange, S., Lomsadze, A., Borodovsky, M. &amp; Stanke, M. BRAKER1: unsupervised RNA-seq-based genome annotation with GeneMark-ET and AUGUSTUS. Bioinformatics 32, 767–769 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3323">100</a></sup>). Because the resulting gene models were heavily dependent on the training data, we decided to generate multiple gene predictions and build consensus gene models using EVidenceModeler (EVM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3327">101</a></sup>. We used both individual approaches (prediction of genes for each genome separately, see (1)–(5) below) and comparative (simultaneous prediction of gene models for the genomes, see (5) below) approaches to increase the accuracy and compatibility of gene annotations. Comparative genome annotation approaches use whole-genome alignment and external evidence (RNA-seq, protein and expressed sequence tag) to simultaneously predict genes in multiple genomes and are able to correct errors may arise during individual-based predictions. The following gene prediction approaches were used. (1) We trained Augustus with only the RNA-seq evidence and predicted gene models by taking into account RNA-seq, protein, Mikado and PASA assembled transcripts. (2) We used the previously trained (in (1)) species model but with a modified weighting file (extrinsic.cfg) to give more weight to the protein evidence. (3) We trained Augustus using both protein and RNA-seq evidence within Braker2 (EPT mode of Braker2). (4) We used the RNA-seq evidence to automatically train genemark and obtain gene predictions. (5) Finally, we ran Augustus in the comparative mode with RNA-seq, transcript and protein evidence and genome alignments inferred by mugsy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Angiuoli, S. V. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Mugsy: fast multiple alignment of closely related whole genomes. Bioinformatics 27, 334–342 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3331">102</a></sup>. Generating this series of genome predictions was necessary as our preliminary analyses suggested that none of the predictions was superior but rather complementary. The proteomes used can be found in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">15</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Generating consensus gene models</h3><p>We used EVM to derive consensus gene models best supported by the various evidence. We used all the previously generated gene predictions (gff files) and selected the best consensus gene models using protein (exonerate-mapped proteomes of species and the uniprot_sport plant dataset) and transcript evidence (Mikado and PASA assembled transcripts). We gave equal weights to each ab initio predictions, transcript evidence (weight 1), but increased the weight for Mikado loci (2) and PASA assembled transcripts (10). After deriving the consensus gene models, we used PASA and the PASA assembled transcripts to correct erroneous gene models, add UTRs (untranslated regions), and predict alternative splice variants in two rounds. Finally, we extensively manually curated these three annotations (revised and corrected various gene models) and used them for all further downstream analyses.</p><p>Our annotation pipeline resulted about 1,000 more predicted gene models for the <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn compared to the Oxford strain. This suggested that despite high collinearity, gene content of the two strains may differ. To aid future comparative analyses we created an <i>A. agrestis</i> pan genome containing a non-redundant set of genomic sequences and annotations of the two strains. Furthermore, we carefully analysed the predicted gene set of the two strains to show that gene number difference is not due to annotation issues. Methods and results of the pan genome construction as well as gene set comparison can be found in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">Supplementary notes</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Genome completeness assessment</h3><p>We used BUSCO v.3 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3366">103</a></sup>) with the Viridiplantae set to assess the completeness of our genomes and annotations. We did not use the Embryophya set because it was constructed based almost exclusively on flowering plant genomes (29 out of 30)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3370">103</a></sup>, which does not offer an appropriate benchmark for non-flowering plant genomes. Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM1">3</a> shows that our genomes have similar (if not better) BUSCO scores compared to many published non-flowering plant genomes. It should be noted that while <i>Physcomitrella</i>, <i>Sphagnum</i> and <i>Marchantia</i> all have much higher BUSCO scores, this is probably reflecting the fact that these genomes were used to compile the Viridiplantae set.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Reconstructing the land plant phylogeny</h3><p>We used Orthofinder2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: phylogenetic orthology inference for comparative genomics. Genome Biol. 20, 238 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3395">104</a></sup>) to identify mostly single-copy genes, with 21 genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#Fig2">2</a>) included in the run to represent angiosperms, ferns, lycophytes, mosses, liverworts, hornworts and the grade of streptophyte algae. A total of 742 mostly single-copy orthogroups were identified. Protein alignments for individual orthogroup were done by MAFFT v.7.427 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Standley, D. M. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 772–780 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3402">105</a></sup>) and back translated to nucleotides by TranslatorX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="Abascal, F., Zardoya, R. &amp; Telford, M. J. TranslatorX: multiple alignment of nucleotide sequences guided by amino acid translations. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, W7–W13 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3406">106</a></sup>. The alignments were processed to remove sites with over 50% gaps or Ns and remove sequences shorter than 50% of the alignment length. When a species had more than one copy in an orthogroup, none from that species was included. To infer gene trees, we used both the amino acid and nucleotide matrices, and employed the maximum likelihood method implemented in IQ-Tree v.1.6.12 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Nguyen, L.-T., Schmidt, H. A., von Haeseler, A. &amp; Minh, B. Q. IQ-TREE: a fast and effective stochastic algorithm for estimating maximum-likelihood phylogenies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 268–274 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3410">107</a></sup>). The best-fitting substitution models were selected by ModelFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 108" title="Kalyaanamoorthy, S., Minh, B. Q., Wong, T. K. F., von Haeseler, A. &amp; Jermiin, L. S. ModelFinder: fast model selection for accurate phylogenetic estimates. Nat. Methods 14, 587–589 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR108" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3415">108</a></sup>. To reduce saturation in nucleotide substitution at this large time scale, the third codon position was excluded.</p><p>To infer the species tree, we used both concatenation and multispecies coalescent approach. The concatenated dataset included all the 742 loci and was analysed using IQ-Tree with ModelFinder model selection. To assess branch supports, we carried out ultrafast bootstrap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 109" title="Hoang, D. T., Chernomor, O., von Haeseler, A., Minh, B. Q. &amp; Vinh, L. S. UFBoot2: improving the ultrafast bootstrap approximation. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 518–522 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR109" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3422">109</a></sup> and SH-aLRT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 110" title="Guindon, S. et al. New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst. Biol. 59, 307–321 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR110" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3426">110</a></sup> analyses (both with 1,000 replicates). For the multispecies coalescent approach, we used ASTRAL-III (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="Zhang, C., Rabiee, M., Sayyari, E. &amp; Mirarab, S. ASTRAL-III: polynomial time species tree reconstruction from partially resolved gene trees. BMC Bioinf. 19, 153 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3430">111</a></sup>) to summarize all the 742 gene trees and measured branch supports as local posterior probabilities<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 112" title="Sayyari, E. &amp; Mirarab, S. Fast coalescent-based computation of local branch support from quartet frequencies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 33, 1654–1668 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR112" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3434">112</a></sup>. Gene-tree/species-tree discordance in terms of quartet frequencies was calculated by DiscoVista<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 113" title="Sayyari, E., Whitfield, J. B. &amp; Mirarab, S. DiscoVista: interpretable visualizations of gene tree discordance. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 122, 110–115 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR113" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3438">113</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Collinearity of the three hornwort genomes and collinearity across Viridiplantae</h3><p>We used the D-GENIES dot-plot tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 114" title="Cabanettes, F. &amp; Klopp, C. D-GENIES: dot plot large genomes in an interactive, efficient and simple way. PeerJ. 6, e4958 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR114" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3450">114</a></sup> with the default options to visually assess collinearity of the three genome assemblies. We also aligned the genomic sequences using the nucmer module of mummer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 115" title="Marçais, G. et al. MUMmer4: a fast and versatile genome alignment system. PLoS Comput. Biol. 14, e1005944 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR115" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3454">115</a></sup> and assessed their differences using Assemblytics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 116" title="Nattestad, M. &amp; Schatz, M. C. Assemblytics: a web analytics tool for the detection of variants from an assembly. Bioinformatics 32, 3021–3023 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR116" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3458">116</a></sup>.</p><p>To study the collinearity across all plants, we first created orthogroups with proteomes of 19 species using Orthofinder2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: phylogenetic orthology inference for comparative genomics. Genome Biol. 20, 238 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3465">104</a></sup>). The dataset included representatives from each major groups of land plants (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">15</a>), and species experienced different numbers of large-scale duplication events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 117" title="Qiao, X. et al. Gene duplication and evolution in recurring polyploidization–diploidization cycles in plants. Genome Biol. 20, 38 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR117" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3472">117</a></sup>. Gff files and proteomes were retrieved from Phytozome v.12 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 118" title="Goodstein, D. M. et al. Phytozome: a comparative platform for green plant genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D1178–D1186 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR118" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3476">118</a></sup>). We used I-ADHore3 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 119" title="Proost, S. et al. i-ADHoRe 3.0—fast and sensitive detection of genomic homology in extremely large data sets. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e11 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR119" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3480">119</a></sup>) to detect highly degenerate collinear blocks among bryophytes and vascular plants requiring a minimum of three, four and five anchor points within each collinear region (gap_size=30, cluster_gap=35, q_value=0.75, prob_cutoff=0.01, anchor_points=5, alignment_method=gg2, level_2_only=false).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec30">Identification of tandem repeats and centromeres</h3><p>We run Tandem Repeats Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 120" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR120" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3492">120</a></sup> to identify tandem repeats with a minimum alignment score of 50 and a maximum period size of 2,000 bp. We then plotted repeat unit size against tandem array size to look for bimodal distribution. To localize centromeric regions in the <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn genome, we generated dot-plots between a short-read-only assembly and the final chromosome-scale assembly. Because centromeric repeats are difficult to assemble using short-reads we expected that they will be missing from the Illumina assembly but will be present in the chromosomal-scale assembly. We also generated a self dot-plot of the <i>A. agrestis</i> Bonn genome to search for regions that are highly similar across scaffolds and are repetitive. Finally, we used the output of Tandem Repeats Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 120" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR120" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3502">120</a></sup> to search for tandem arrays with a period length of minimum 10 bp and with a minimum tandem array length of 30 repeat units. We plotted the location of these tandem arrays along the chromosomes to visually assessed their distribution.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec31">Screening for whole-genome duplication</h3><p>We used a combination of synonymous divergence (Ks) and synteny analyses to look for evidence of whole-genome duplication in the <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes. For each genome, we used the DupPipe pipeline to construct gene families and estimate the age of gene duplications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 121" title="Barker, M. S. et al. EvoPipes.net: bioinformatic tools for ecological and evolutionary genomics. Evol. Bioinform. 6, 143–149 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR121" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3518">121</a></sup>. We translated DNA sequences and identified reading frames by comparing the Genewise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 122" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. Genewise and genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR122" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3522">122</a></sup> alignment to the best-hit protein from a collection of proteins from 25 plant genomes from Phytozome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 118" title="Goodstein, D. M. et al. Phytozome: a comparative platform for green plant genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D1178–D1186 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR118" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3526">118</a></sup>. For each analysis, we used protein-guided DNA alignments to align our nucleic acid sequences while maintaining reading frame. We then used single-linkage clustering to construct gene families and estimate K<sub>S</sub> divergence using phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood (PAML)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 123" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR123" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3533">123</a></sup> with the F3X4 model for each node in the gene family phylogenies. Because the <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes contain large numbers of pentatricopeptide repeat genes (PPR), we also repeated the analysis with all the PPR genes removed. PPR genes were identified based on the Orthofinder results (see later).</p><p>For synteny analysis, we used MCscan’s ‘jcvi.compara.catalog ortholog’<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 124" title="Tang, H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiply-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944–1954 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR124" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3543">124</a></sup> function to search for and visualize intragenomic syntenic regions. The default <i>C</i>-score of 0.7 is used to filter low-quality hits. To calculate syntenic depths, the ‘jcvi.compara.synteny depth’ function was used. For comparison, we also carried out the same analysis for <i>P. patens</i> v.3.3 and <i>M. polymorpha</i> v.3.0 genomes; the former is known to have two rounds of WGD while the latter has none<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287–299 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3556">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Lang, D. et al. The Physcomitrella patens chromosome-scale assembly reveals moss genome structure and evolution. Plant J. 93, 515–533 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3559">21</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec32">Transcription factor annotation</h3><p>TAPs were annotated using TAPscan, according to Wilhelmsson et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Wilhelmsson, P. K. I., Mühlich, C., Ullrich, K. K. &amp; Rensing, S. A. Comprehensive genome-wide classification reveals that many plant-specific transcription factors evolved in streptophyte algae. Genome Biol. Evol. 9, 3384–3397 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3571">24</a></sup> and compared with selected other organisms using the major protein of each gene model (‘.1’ splice variant). TF annotations were further manually checked and adjusted for annotation errors or missing annotations.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec33">Gene family classification and curation</h3><p>We used Orthofinder2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: phylogenetic orthology inference for comparative genomics. Genome Biol. 20, 238 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3583">104</a></sup>) to classify gene families of 25 plant and algal complete genomes, including the three hornworts reported here (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">16</a>) into orthogroups. Orthofinder was run using the default setting, except that the ‘msa’ option was used. A total of 31,001 orthogroups were circumscribed. The detailed gene count and classification results can be found in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#MOESM3">16</a>. While Orthofinder2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: phylogenetic orthology inference for comparative genomics. Genome Biol. 20, 238 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3593">104</a></sup>) provides an automatic circumscription of gene families, they rarely correspond to their expert-based circumscriptions and can contain a substantial number of misclassified gene models due to the inherent limitations of the automatic classification algorithms. Therefore, all Orthofinder2 generated gene families selected for detailed evolutionary analyses were manually curated by their experts. In particular, members of all extensively investigated gene families were checked for the presence of their domain structure either using InterPro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 125" title="Mitchell, A. L. et al. InterPro in 2019: improving coverage, classification and access to protein sequence annotations. Nucleic Acids Res. 47, D351–D360 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR125" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3597">125</a></sup>, Pfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 126" title="El-Gebali, S. et al. The Pfam protein families database in 2019. Nucleic Acids Res. 47, D427–D432 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR126" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3602">126</a></sup> or CCD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 127" title="Marchler-Bauer, A. et al. CDD/SPARCLE: functional classification of proteins via subfamily domain architectures. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, D200–D203 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR127" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3606">127</a></sup> to remove false positives and/or correct improperly predicted gene models. Furthermore, to ensure that a gene is truly absent (and not just unannotated), we carried out additional searches on the genome assemblies. For each extensively analysed gene family, we directly searched the raw genomic sequence using bryophyte or vascular plant homologues as query sequences to find additional gene models that might have been missed by our gene prediction pipeline. These searches were done using tBLASTn<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 128" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinf. 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR128" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3610">128</a></sup> and, in case no hit was found, were repeated with the hmmsearch module of HMMER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 129" title="Wheeler, T. J. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Nhmmer: DNA homology search with profile HMMs. Bioinformatics 29, 2487–2489 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR129" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3614">129</a></sup> using the corresponding hmmer profiles from Pfam. Indeed, the manual curation helped us to add, revise and correct a substantial number of existing and/or missing gene models. Therefore, we believe that our careful manual curation ensures that the number of false positives and negatives are kept low and allows us to make statements about the presence/absence of particular genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec34">Phylogenetic reconstruction of KNOX, LEAFY, WOX and YABBY</h3><p>For KNOX, AagrBONN.evm.model.Sc2ySwM.368.1986.6 was used as a query to BLASTp search at NCBI on 13 Sept 2019. The search database was NCBI non-redundant (nr) database limited to records that include: <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i> (japonica cultivar-group), <i>Phalaenopsis equestris</i>, <i>Amborella trichopoda</i>, <i>Ceratopteris richardii</i>, <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i>, <i>M. polymorpha</i>, <i>P. patens</i>, <i>K. nitens</i>, <i>Ostreococcus tauri</i> and <i>C. reinhardtii</i>. The search parameters were otherwise as default. The hit sequences were downloaded and combined with the <i>Anthoceros</i> KNOX sequences, then aligned with FFT-NS-2 in MAFFT v.7.427 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Standley, D. M. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 772–780 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3664">105</a></sup>). The alignment was manually inspected in Mesquite v.3.6 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 130" title="Maddison, W. P. &amp; Maddison, D. R. Mesquite: A Modular System for Evolutionary Analysis v.3.04 (Mesquite, accessed 5 July 2016); &#xA;http://mesquiteproject.org&#xA;&#xA;&#xA;" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR130" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3668">130</a></sup>) and 149 well-conserved sites of 51 sequences were included. Phylogenetic analysis based on maximum likelihood (ML) was conducted in MEGA X<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 131" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G., Li, M., Knyaz, C. &amp; Tamura, K. MEGA X: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis across computing platforms. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 1547–1549 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR131" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3672">131</a></sup>. The best-fitting model was chosen as LG+G+I using the FindBestProteinModel function. A total of 100 bootstrap replicates were performed to evaluate branch support. ‘ML Heuristic Method’ was set to ‘Subtree-Pruning-Regrafting – Extensive (SPR level 5)’ and ‘No. of Discrete Gamma Categories’ set to 5.</p><p>For LEAFY, AagrOXF evm.model.utg000049l.76.4 was used as a query to BLASTp search at NCBI on 30 August 2019. The search database was nr limited to records that include: <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>O. sativa</i> (japonica cultivar-group), <i>P. equestris</i>, <i>A. trichopoda</i>, <i>P. radiata</i>, <i>P. armandii</i>, <i>P. abies</i>, <i>C. richardii</i>, <i>S. moellendorffii</i>, <i>M. polymorpha</i> and <i>P. patens</i>. The search parameters were otherwise as default. The hit sequences were downloaded and combined with the <i>Anthoceros</i> LEAFY sequence and AHJ90704.1, AHJ90706.1, AHJ90707.1 from Sayou et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 132" title="Sayou, C. et al. A promiscuous intermediate underlies the evolution of LEAFY DNA binding specificity. Science 343, 645–648 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR132" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3717">132</a></sup>, then aligned with FFT-NS-2 in MAFFT v.7.427 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Standley, D. M. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 772–780 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3721">105</a></sup>). The alignment was manually inspected and processed as described above to include 194 conserved sites of 20 sequences. Phylogenetic inference was done similarly as above but with LG selected as the best-fitting model.</p><p>For WOX, WOX genes in <i>Anthoceros</i> genomes were searched using the corresponding <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>P. patens</i> and <i>M. polymorpha</i> proteins. Based on comparison among the three genomes, three gene models with excess intron predictions were manually revised and one model was added. AagrOXF_evm.model.utg000018l.552.1 was used as a query to BLASTp search at NCBI on 9 October 2019. The search database was nr limited to records that include: <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>O. sativa</i> (japonica cultivar-group), <i>P. equestris</i>, <i>A. trichopoda</i>, <i>C. richardii</i>, <i>S. moellendorffii</i>, <i>M. polymorpha</i>, <i>P. patens</i>, <i>K. nitens</i> and <i>C. braunii</i>. Maximum target was set to 250 and the word size as 2. The search parameters were otherwise as default. The hit sequences were downloaded and combined with the <i>Anthoceros</i> WOX sequences, then aligned with einsi —maxiterate 1,000 in MAFFT v.7.429 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Standley, D. M. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 772–780 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3775">105</a></sup>). The alignment was manually inspected with Mesquite v.3.6 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 130" title="Maddison, W. P. &amp; Maddison, D. R. Mesquite: A Modular System for Evolutionary Analysis v.3.04 (Mesquite, accessed 5 July 2016); &#xA;http://mesquiteproject.org&#xA;&#xA;&#xA;" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR130" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3779">130</a></sup>) and a matrix consisting of 58 included sites of 142 sequences was constructed. Sequences identical in the included region were treated as a single operational taxonomic unit (OTU) during the phylogenetic analysis. The best-fitting model was chosen as JTT with ProteinModelSelection8.pl. The maximum likelihood (ML) tree was inferred by RAxM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 133" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR133" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3784">133</a></sup> with -f a -\# 100 -m PROTGAMMAJTT and supplying -p and -x from random number generator. Bootstrap samples were generated with seqboot from PHYLIP package v.3.697 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 134" title="Felsenstein, J. PHYLIP (Phylogeny Inference Package) Version 3.697 (University of Washington, 2015); &#xA;http://evolution.genetics.washington.edu/phylip/oldversions.html&#xA;&#xA;&#xA;" href="/articles/s41477-020-0618-2#ref-CR134" id="ref-link-section-d57167e3788">134</a></sup>) and RAxML was run for each of them.</p><p>For <i>YABBY</i>, the 107 OTU dataset from Finet et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 135" title="Finet, C. et al. Evolution of the YABBY gene family in seed plants. Evol.
